Milley, USA, For Reappointment to the Grade of General and to be Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff', Senate Armed Services Committee, 11 July 2019, 64-66.\n$^{126}$ National Cyber Strategy of the United States of America, The White House, September 2018.\n#\nE. D. BORGHARD AND S. W. LONERGAN\nSecurity Strategy. $^{127}$  Therefore, if defend forward is meant to be part of a deterrence strategy anchored in denial, the U.S. government should clarify it as such. $^{128}$\nA related challenge is that defend forward is often conflated with simply going on the offense in cyberspace and, by extension, punishment strategies. For instance, then-Secretary of Defense Mark Esper, testifying to Congress in July 2019 about the Trump administration's issuance of National Security Presidential Memorandum 13 on offensive cyber operations, noted that, \"last year the [sic] put out a new NSPM-13, which really put our cyber capabilities on a more offensive footing, allowing us [sic] lean forward,\" and noted that, \"[o]n the offensive side, I think we have a lot of capabilities, but policy had not caught until, as you [sic] the passage of NSPM-13.\" More recently, Colin Kahl, Undersecretary of Defense for Policy, noted in his 2021 confirmation hearing that deterrence by punishment is the \"cyber equivalent\" of the adversary knowing that the U.S. will \"strike back in a way that will hurt.\" While a denial strategy may contain offensive elements, they are meant to be circumscribed to a specific set of targets. In contrast, ambiguity about this risks creating the conditions for offensive cyber operations to be employed against a range of targets for a variety of strategic purposes, rather than more narrowly defined to encompass the set of offensive capabilities and infrastructure that adversaries rely on for the types of cyber operations that the U.S. is trying to reduce. Put simply, just as deterrent defense is not synonymous with pure defense, deterrence by denial is not simply interchangeable with offense. Clarifying the left and right limits of defend forward and its relation to broader strategic concepts is essential for its success.\n$^{127}$ John S. McCain National Defense Authorization Act for Fiscal Year 2019, H.R. 5515, 115th Congress, 2018, Section 1652. 'Summary: Department of Defense Cyber Strategy', 2018; and 'National Security Strategy of the United States of America', Executive Office of the President, December 2017.\n$^{128}$ The March 2020 U.S. Cyberspace Solarium Commission report specifically recommends that this should occur through the Executive branch issuing an updated national cyber strategy that includes this discussion. Borghard and Mark Montgomery, 'Defend Forward as a Whole-of-Nation Effort', Lawfare, 11 March 2019, https://www.lawfareblog.com/defend-forward-whole-nation-effort.\n$^{130}$ Stenographic Transcript Before the Committee on Armed Services, United States Senate Hearing to Conduct a Confirming Hearing on the Expected Nomination of: Honorable Mark T. Esper to be Secretary of Defense, 16 July 2019, Washington, D.C., 37, 60. Also see Ellen Nakashima, 'White House Authorizes \"Offensive Cyber Operations\" to Deter Foreign Adversaries', Washington Post, 20 September 2018, https://www.washingtonpost.com/world/national-security/trump-authorizes-offensive-cyber-operations-to-deter-foreign-adversaries-bolton-says/2018/09/20/b5880578-bd0b-11e8-b7d2-0773aa1e33da_story..\n$^{131}$ 'Hearing to Consider the Nomination of Dr. Colin H. Kahl To Be Under Secretary of Defense for Policy', Committee on Armed Services, March 2021, p. 72.\nConclusion\nThe binary debate about whether cyber deterrence is feasible misses more nuanced questions about the thresholds at which deterrence may operate, how deterrence strategies may manifest differently at different levels, and how deterrence outcomes may be different in cyberspace than in other areas, particularly the nuclear realm. Moreover, cyber deterrence in general and deterrence by denial in particular have come to mean so many different things -- ranging from offense, defense, and resilience, and including nearly every instrument of power a state has at its disposal -- that they have lost conceptual rigor. This article set out to clarify the application of deterrence theory to cyberspace and revive the concept of deterrence by denial through drawing on the traditional denial literature, especially the conventional deterrence literature. We aimed to impose limits and conceptual clarity on how a deterrence by denial strategy could be implemented in cyberspace. Moreover, there are elements of U.S. cyber strategy, specifically the DoD's concept of defend forward, that could provide a foundation for implementing deterrence by denial, but are constrained by significant conceptual challenges.\nThat said, deterrence by denial still has conceptual utility, especially because it helps to clarify a state's strategic objectives. Counter-cyber operations to degrade adversary capabilities could be employed in support of a range of strategic objectives -- prevention, coercion, simply brute force. Therefore, a strategic framework that guides the employment of military capabilities is important. If policymakers ultimately seek to implement a deterrence by denial approach in cyberspace, it will require strategic clarity and discipline so that it does not become yet another empty strategic concept without clearly defined objectives and limitations.\n## Acknowledgements\nThe authors would like to thank the Commissioners and staff of the U.S. Cyberspace Solarium Commission, on which they both served, who significantly contributed to their thinking about deterrence by denial strategies in cyberspace and their policy implications. The views expressed in this article are personal and do not reflect the policy or position of any U.S. government entity or organization.\n## Disclosure statement\nNo potential conflict of interest was reported by the authors."

Lonergan (2023) Deterrence by denial in cyberspace, Journal of Strategic Studies, 46:3, 534-569, DOI: 10.1080/01402390.2021.1944856 To link to this article: https://doi.org/10.1080/01402390.2021.1944856 Published online: 03 Aug 2021.\nSubmit your article to this journal Article views: 3265 View related articles Search View Crossmark data Citing articles: 6 View citing articles Full Terms &amp; Conditions of access and use can be found at https://www.tandfonline.com/action/journalInformation?journalCode=fjss20 JOURNAL OF STRATEGIC STUDIES 2023, VOL. 46, NO. 3, 534-569 https://doi.org/10.1080/01402390.2021.1944856 # Routledge Taylor &amp; Francis Group ARTICLE Check for updates # Deterrence by denial in cyberspace Erica D. Borgharda and Shawn W. Lonerganb $^{a}$ Carnegie Endowment for International Peace, Columbia University; $^{b}$ 75th Innovation Command and PricewaterhouseCoopers # ABSTRACT Many scholars and practitioners are unconvinced that cyber deterrence is possible. This article aims to demonstrate why some of this skepticism is misplaced, as well as provide greater clarity and conceptual rigor to the proliferation of arguments within the United States about deterrence in cyberspace. Specifically, we argue that cyber deterrence frameworks that draw from the traditional nuclear deterrence literature and the logic of deterrence by punishment are mismatched to deterrence challenges in cyberspace. Instead, we advocate for a deterrence by denial approach, but one that is oriented around counter-cyber operations rather than simply improving defenses. While there has been some scholarship and work by practitioners that implicitly rests on a deterrence by denial logic, they suffer from a lack of systematic assessment of how traditional denial concepts, especially those developed in the conventional deterrence literature, could be extended to cyberspace. Therefore, in this article, we review different deterrence concepts in theory and practice, articulate a logic of cyber deterrence by denial, and provide policy recommendations for the United States.\nKEYWORDS Deterrence theory; cyberspace; U.S. cyber strategy and policy # Introduction Many scholars and practitioners are unconvinced that cyber deterrence is possible. This article aims to demonstrate why some of this skepticism is misplaced, as well as provide greater clarity and conceptual rigor to the proliferation of arguments within the United States about deterrence in cyberspace. Specifically, we argue that cyber deterrence frameworks that draw from the traditional nuclear deterrence literature and the logic of deterrence by punishment are mismatched to deterrence challenges in cyberspace. Instead, we advocate for a deterrence by denial approach, but one that is oriented around counter-cyber operations rather than simply improving defenses. While there has been some scholarship and work by practitioners that implicitly rests on a deterrence by denial logic, they suffer from a lack of systematic assessment of how traditional denial concepts, CONTACT Erica D. Borghard eborghard@gmail.com Carnegie Endowment for International Peace, Columbia University © 2021 Informa UK Limited, trading as Taylor &amp; Francis Group THE JOURNAL OF STRATEGIC STUDIES\n\n## Evolution of the cyber deterrence literature\nContemporary cyber deterrence scholarship, similar to the traditional deterrence literature, has evolved in waves.¹ The first, spanning the early-2000s to the mid-2010s, heavily borrows from the nuclear deterrence literature, and leverages nuclear weapons as an analogy to cyber weapons. This literature applies concepts such as the security dilemma, the offense-defense balance, and counter-value versus counter-force targeting to the cyber-domain.² Akin to nuclear weapons, this literature characterizes the advent of cyber weapons as heralding a revolution in warfare with significant and systemic implications.³ The central research question for these scholars is whether deterrence is even possible in cyberspace. Sometimes explicitly, but usually implicitly, cyber deterrence refers to reciprocal, within-domain deterrence; that is, the threat of employing cyber capabilities to prevent an adversary from doing the same.⁴ Moreover, deterrence by punishment – the credible\n¹Robert Jervis, ‘Deterrence Theory Revisited’, *World Politics* 31/2 , 289–324. Jeffrey Knopf identifies a ‘fourth wave’ of deterrence theory that addressed asymmetric threats in a post-9/11 international system. See Jeffrey W. Knopf, ‘The Fourth Wave in Deterrence Research’, *Contemporary Security Policy* 31/1 (2010), 1–33. For a review of deterrence theory, see Austin G. Long, *Deterrence: From Cold War to Long War* (Santa Monica, CA: RAND Corporation, 2008).\n²See, for example, Martin Libicki’s *Cyberdeterrence and Cyberwar* (Santa Monica: RAND Corporation, 2009); Gregory J. Rattray, *Strategic Warfare in Cyberspace* (Massachusetts: The MIT Press, 2001); Proceedings of a Workshop on Deterring Cyber Attacks, particularly the collection of chapters under ‘Group 2’ that address strategy, policy, and doctrine. LONERGAN\nthreat to wreak devastating pain and suffering on a target's civilian population such that the perceived costs of an action are perceived to be unacceptably high – is the prominent reference point.[5]\nThis early cyber deterrence literature identifies a number of purportedly unique or distinct characteristics of the cyber domain that are hypothesized to have implications for the successful functioning of deterrence, such as problems of attribution, low barriers to entry, the proliferation of state and non-state actors, the advantage of offense over defense, the speed with which cyber attacks take place, or the absence of borders and boundaries.[6] These scholars' basic conclusion is that cyber deterrence is highly problematic, if not impossible.[7] On its face, the empirical record appears to support this skepticism. Academics and think tanks have collected data documenting the proliferation of hundreds of cyber operations over the past few decades, including significant offensive cyber operations that have targeted critical infrastructure and caused major economic effects (such as Russia's cyber attack against Ukraine's power grid in 2015 and 2016, the Stuxnet cyber attack targeting Iran's nuclear weapons program, Iran's distributed denial of service attacks against the U.S. financial system from 2012-2013, or the 2017 NotPetya cyber attack, attributed to Russia, that unleashed destructive malware around the world).\nThe second wave of cyber deterrence theory, which emerged in the mid-2010s, takes the feasibility of cyber deterrence in two distinct directions, one chartered by what we refer to as \"deep deterrence pessimists\" and the other by a diverse group of \"cautious deterrence optimists.\" The deterrence pessimists, epitomized by Richard Harknett and Michael Fischerkeller's work, effectively throw the deterrence baby out with the bathwater. Harknett and Fischerkeller argue that specific attributes of cyberspace, especially the absence of traditional sovereignty and an environment of \"constant contact,\" mean the \"protection or advancement of national interests cannot rest on deterrence as the central strategy.\" They argue that this has critical\n[5] Glenn Snyder, *Deterrence and Defense* (Princeton, NJ: Princeton University Press, 1961), 14–15; Allan Friedman and P.W. Singer, *Cybersecurity and Cyberwar: What Everyone Needs to Know* (New York, NY: Oxford University Press, 2014).\n[6] Some of the unique characteristics of the cyber domain are due to the global, interdependent, and effectively borderless nature of cyberspace, where adversary activities occur across traditional sovereign boundaries. Furthermore, some early cyber deterrence scholars believed these unique characteristics enabled the offense to possess the ‘upper-hand.’ For further analysis, see the following: Michael P. Fischerkeller and Richard Harknett, ‘Deterrence is Not a Credible Strategy for Cyberspace’, *Orbis* 61/3 (2017), 381–393; Rebecca Slayton, ‘What is the Cyber Offense-Defense Balance? Conceptions, Causes, and Assessment’, *International Security* 41/3 (2017), 72–109; and Thomas Rid and Ben Buchanan, ‘Attributing Cyber Attacks’, *Journal of Strategic Studies* 38/1–2 (2015), 4–37.\n[7] Fischerkeller and Harknett, ‘Deterrence is Not a Credible Strategy’, Libicki, Cyber Deterrence and Cyberwar; Joseph S. Nye, Jr., ‘Deterrence and Dissuasion in Cyberspace’, *International Security* 41/3 (2017), 44–71; and Fischerkeller and Harknett explicitly contend deterrence is not possible subthreshold in cyberspace because of what they identify as inherent attributes of the cyberspace environment.\n[8] Fischerkeller and Harknett, ‘Deterrence is Not a Credible Strategy’, 382.\nTHE JOURNAL OF STRATEGIC STUDIES\n\nConversely, the guardedly optimistic camp sought to refine and caveat the early cyber deterrence literature, salvaging deterrence in cyberspace where possible. For example, Jacquelyn Schneider, Erik Gartzke, and Jon Lindsay situate cyber capabilities within a broader context by focusing on the logic of cross-domain deterrence – the employment of tools across domains of warfare to deter cyber attacks as well as the use of cyber operations for non-cyber objectives10; Joseph Nye, Henry Farrell, and Abraham Newman explore how interdependence and entanglement of actors in cyberspace could have deterrent effects11; Gartzke and Lindsay further explore how the role of deception in cyberspace could aid rather than undermine deterrence12; and Schneider and Nye both explore the extent to which deterrence by denial, rather than punishment, may be technically feasible in cyberspace.13 Many U.S. practitioners have echoed this cautious optimism, which is also reflected in U.S. strategy and policy. For instance, during his 2018 Senate confirmation hearing, then-Lieutenant General Paul Nakasone noted that he “believe[s] it is possible for actions in cyberspace to have a deterrent effect and contribute to the Nation’s overall deterrence posture,” but also noted that “[t]he current level and tempo of cyber attacks is not tolerable.”14 Similarly, the March 2020 report of the U.S. Cyberspace Solarium Commission, established in the 2019 National Defense Authorization Act to develop a U.S. strategy for cyberspace, articulates a strategy of “layered cyber deterrence” that “combines a number of traditional deterrence mechanisms and extends them beyond the government to develop a whole-of-nation approach.”15 At the same time, the report depicts an environment in which the United States has not deterred a range of malicious cyber behavior, noting that “the United States has not sufficiently changed the cost-benefit calculus made by adversaries when determining whether or not to attack in\n\n# E. D. BORGHARD AND S. W. LONERGAN cyberspace below the level of armed conflict.\"¹⁶ Other strategy documents, ranging from the 2017 Defense Science Board's Task Force on Cyber Deterrence to the Trump administration's 2018 National Cyber Strategy, are also explicitly anchored in deterrence and advocate for deterrence-based strategies, while at the same time implying or stating that U.S. deterrence has not been successful.¹⁷ We build on this latter body of cautious deterrence optimist scholarship to argue that deterrence by denial in cyberspace is possible and further articulate the logic and implementation of a denial strategy. Specifically, we argue that the evolution of the cyber deterrence literature outlined above suffers from a problem of scholarly path dependence. Early emphasis in the literature on comparisons to the nuclear deterrence literature, particularly deterrence by punishment concepts, confers too much prominence to the latter ideas at the expense of the conventional deterrence literature, which is anchored in deterrence by denial.¹⁸ In revitalizing the conventional deterrence literature, we provide a framework for cyber deterrence by denial and articulate the mechanisms through which different strategic and operational concepts could serve denial objectives, with a particular focus on cyber strategy in the United States. We also explore the relationship between\"defense\"as a standalone concept versus as a subset of deterrence by denial, and show why conflating these concepts risks overlooking the offensive elements of denial.¹⁹ As part of our analysis, we map different deterrence concepts in U.S. cyber strategy and policy onto existing frameworks. We argue that a lack of clarity about cyber deterrence strategies inevitably produced conceptual confusion. Finally, we describe the core elements of a deterrence by denial strategy for cyberspace and identify the gap between existing U.S. approaches that are suggestive of deterrence by denial – particularly the U.S. Department of Defense (DoD)'s 2018 concept of\"defend forward\"– and our denial concept.²⁰ ## Deterrence by denial and conventional deterrence In making a case for deterrence by denial in cyberspace, we draw from the traditional deterrence by denial literature and, in particular, the literature on conventional rather than nuclear deterrence. The former literature contains three applications to cyberspace. First, at a fundamental level, the logic of ¹⁶ 'United States of America Cyberspace Solarium Commission', March 2020, pp. 14–15.\n¹⁷ 'Task Force on Cyber Deterrence', Department of Defense, Defense Science Board. 'National Cyber Strategy of the United States of America'.\n¹⁸ A similar critique has historically been leveled at deterrence theorists. See, for example, George H. Quester, Deterrence Before Hiroshima: The Airpower Background of Modern Strategy (Routledge Publishers, 1986).\n¹⁹ Robert J. Art, 'To What Ends Military Power?' International Security 4/4 (1980), 3–35.\n²⁰ 'Summary: Department of Defense Cyber Strategy', U.S. Department of Defense, January 2018.\nTHE JOURNAL OF STRATEGIC STUDIES\n\n## Denial versus punishment\nDeterrence is a coercive strategy that seeks to prevent an actor from taking an unacceptable action; it is the “power to dissuade.”²³ Robert Art defines deterrence as “the deployment of military power so as to be able to prevent an adversary from doing something that one does not want him to do and that he otherwise might be tempted to do by threatening him with unacceptable punishment if he does it.”²⁴ Deterrence succeeds when the target perceives that the costs and risks of a military action outweigh the expected gains.²⁵ The literature distinguishes between two variants of deterrence: denial and punishment.²⁶ Punishment “involves threatening to destroy large portions of an opponent’s civilian population and industry,” while denial “requires convincing an opponent that he will not attain his goals on the battlefield.”²⁷ In the context of nuclear weapons, deterrence by punishment involves counter value targeting against the adversary’s population centers.²⁸\nIn contrast, deterrence by denial involves threatening to physically impede an adversary’s ability to successfully carry out a military operation or making it too costly to do so. This form of deterrence works by targeting an adversary’s military capabilities or shoring up one’s own defenses such that offensive\n²¹ There are other mechanisms of deterrence beyond punishment and denial, such as deterrence by entanglement or deterrence by normative taboos, which have also been extended to cyberspace. See Nye, ‘Deterrence and Dissuasion’, However, to scope our analysis, we exclude these.\n²² However, it is important to note that recent research has demonstrated that Cold War nuclear strategies were not solely organized around punishment via countervalue strikes, and also included damage limitation and counterforce strategies. We are grateful to Reviewer 1 for this insight.\n²³ Glenn Snyder, *Deterrence and Defense*, 9; and Lawrence Freedman, *Deterrence* (Cambridge, UK: Polity, 2004), 26.\n²⁴ Art, ‘To What Ends Military Power?’ However, it is difficult to ascertain whether a given deterrent threat was successful because deterrence has a negative objective. See the following for debates about the efficacy of deterrence strategy during the Cold War: Robert Jervis, *The Illogic of American Nuclear Strategy* (Ithaca, NY: Cornell University Press, 1984); Lawrence Freedman, *The Evolution of Nuclear Strategy* (London, UK: Palgrave Macmillan, 1989); Long, *Deterrence*; and Francis J. Gavin, *Nuclear Statecraft: History and Strategy in America’s Atomic Age* (Ithaca, NY: Cornell University Press, 2012).\n²⁵ John J. Mearsheimer, ‘Nuclear Weapons and Deterrence in Europe’, *International Security* 9/3 (1984), 21.\n²⁶ The addition of deterrence by entanglement and norms did not come until much later and is beyond a pure definition of deterrence, according to Nye in ‘Deterrence and Dissuasion in Cyberspace’.\n²⁷ John J. Mearsheimer, *Conventional Deterrence* (Ithaca, NY: Cornell University Press, 1985), 14–15.\n²⁸ Freedman, *The Evolution of Nuclear Strategy*, 83.\n#\nE. D. BORGHARD AND S. W. LONERGAN\noperations are perceived to be inordinately costly for an attacking state. $^{29}$  In related formulations, denial is \"preventing battlefield success,\" $^{30}$  and denial strategies aim to \"deny the opponent hope of achieving the disputed territorial objectives. Thus, denial campaigns focus on the target state's military strategy.\" $^{31}$  However, denial succeeds not only when a state can thwart an adversary's military operations, but also when it can target the capabilities on which its military objectives rely as well as its overall military strategy. $^{32}$  While the primary difference between denial and punishment is the nature of the target, there are other important distinctions between these deterrence mechanisms, such as denial being more credible and also calculable because it rests on assessing the deterring state's capabilities more so than its intention. $^{33}$\nWhile the logic of denial has been applied in the nuclear context, it is central to conventional deterrence strategies.[34] Conventional deterrence is \"a function of the capability of denying an aggressor his battlefield objectives with conventional forces.\"[35] Specifically, conventional deterrence works when the attacking states perceives that the probability of achieving its objectives on the battlefield is low and that the costs are likely to be high.[36] In the conventional realm, the efficacy of deterrence is conditional on two variables: first, the costs the attacker would have to pay in material resources, military and civilian casualties, and other related costs associated with the\n29Glenn Snyder, Deterrence and Defense, 14-15.\n30Daniel Byman and Matthew Waxman, The Dynamics of Coercion: American Foreign Policy and the Limits of Military Might (Cambridge, UK: Cambridge University Press, 2002), 50. Importantly, their discussion of denial is in the context of coercion rather than deterrence, but deterrence is a coercive strategy and has a shared logic.\n31Robert A. Pape, Bombing to Win: Air Power and Coercion in War (Ithaca, NY: Cornell University Press, 1996), 19. While this is also a discussion of coercion, the same logic holds for deterrence.\n32Byman and Waxman, The Dynamics of Coercion, 79; and Pape, Bombing to Win, 29.\n33Snyder, Deterrence and Defense, 15-16; and Samuel P. Huntington makes a similar point in 'Conventional Deterrence and Conventional Retaliation in Europe', International Security 8/3 (1983), 35.\n34For denial in a nuclear context, see, for example, Jack L. Gray, 'Theater Nuclear Weapons: Doctrines and Postures', World Politics 28/2 , 305; Mearsheimer, 'Nuclear Weapons and Deterrence in Europe,' and Bernard Brodie's discussion of tactical nuclear weapons in 'The Development of Nuclear Strategy', International Security 2/4 , 76-78. For conventional deterrence by denial, see, for example, Mearsheimer, Conventional Deterrence; Colin S. Gray, 'War-Fighting for Deterrence', The Journal of Strategic Studies 7/1 (1984), 5-28. Fearon, 'Rationalist Explanations for War', International Organization 49/3 (1995), 379-414.\nTHE JOURNAL OF STRATEGIC STUDIES\n\n## Defense and denial as distinct components of deterrence by denial\nDeterrence by denial is sometimes erroneously conflated with the concept of defense. However, these are distinct concepts. While defense can be one mechanism that contributes to deterrence by denial, it is not the only – or even primary – means of achieving denial. Defense is a distinct strategic concept in its own right. Robert Art defines the defensive use of force as “the deployment of military power … to ward off an attack and to minimize damage to oneself if attacked.”⁴¹ Thomas Schelling terms this “forcible defense” or “pure defense,” in which the objective is to “resist successfully, so that the enemy cannot succeed even if he tries.”⁴² While possessing strong defensive capabilities may, in itself, deter aggression by affecting the opponents’ calculus regarding the expected costs of war, it is not synonymous with denial capabilities. Denial entails the “capability to hold territory in case of attack,” while defensive capabilities protect a state’s security if deterrence fails.⁴³\nMilitary capabilities that can threaten another state’s military capabilities used to achieve their strategic objectives (denial) may not overlap perfectly with capabilities that can mitigate the consequences of enemy aggression (defense).⁴⁴ This is why, Glenn Snyder notes, “capabilities and threats which\n³⁷ Mearsheimer, Conventional Deterrence, 23.\n³⁸ Ibid., 24.\n³⁹ Huntington, ‘Conventional Deterrence’, 35–36.\n⁴⁰ In particular, Mearsheimer identifies three strategies of conventional deterrence: attrition, blitzkrieg, and limited aims. Mearsheimer, Conventional Deterrence, 29–30.\n⁴¹ Art, ‘To What Ends Military Power?’ 5. Although, interestingly, Art’s definition of deterrence is entirely focused on deterrence by punishment and does not conceive of deterrence by denial.\n⁴² Thomas C. Schelling, Arms and Influence (New Haven, CT: Yale University Press, 1966), 78.\n⁴³ Snyder, Deterrence and Defense, p. 30 fn. 21, and p. 31.\n⁴⁴ Ibid, 3–5.\n#\nE. D. BORGHARD AND S. W. LONERGAN\nproduce a high level of deterrence may not yield a high degree of security.\"45 Samuel Huntington also observes that conventional defense can contribute to conventional deterrence, but they are not necessarily interchangeable.46 For Schelling, the distinction between denial and defense is centered on force structure and the intent of force employment. If the intent were to ensure an attacking enemy could not succeed, it would constitute defense. However, \"[i] f the object is to induce ... [the enemy] not to proceed, by making his encroachment painful or costly, we can ... call it ... 'deterrent' defense.\"47 Definitions of denial capabilities and Schelling's \"deterrent defense\" are both organized around imposing costs on the military capabilities an adversary employs to accomplish strategic objectives with the goal of convincing an opponent that an offensive military action would be too costly to pursue, whereas defense is about survival.\n# Continuous versus binary-dependent variables\nFinally, an important difference between conventional and nuclear deterrence is how the dependent variable is conceptualized. In general, deterrence failures are observable when the deterring state has to resort to force once the opponent has defected.[48] However, in the context of nuclear deterrence, this outcome is starkly binary. A single failure of deterrence that results in the employment of nuclear weapons (particularly strategic nuclear capabilities) would have devastating consequences. In contrast, the outcome variable is more ambiguous for conventional deterrence and denial strategies. For instance, Snyder argues that deterrence by denial can operate both prior to and during warfighting, particularly during limited wars.[49] In this context, the application of force could deter further aggression or incursion by an opponent. Schelling discusses the concept of \"graduated deterrence\" or \"active deterrence\" for conventional deterrence, in which force could be used for the purposes of \"demonstrating by resistance that the conquest will be costly.\"[50] Jonathan Shimshoni argues that the robustness of a state's deterrence is established over time and, therefore, a state may have to employ force in some circumstances to develop a strong deterrent reputation, including waging limited wars. This use of force does not represent a failure of\n45Ibid, 31.\n46Huntington, 'Conventional Deterrence', 36. Huntington notes that many people neglect the distinction between the concepts of 'defense' and 'retaliation' on page 37 of his work.\n47Schelling, Arms and Influence, 78-79.\n48Richard Ned Lebow and Janice Gross Stein, 'Deterrence: The Elusive Dependent Variable', World Politics 42/3 (1990), 336-369; and Paul Huth and Bruce Russett, 'Deterrence Failure and Crisis Escalation', International Studies Quarterly 32/1 (1998), 29-45.\n49Snyder, Deterrence and Defense, 11.\n50Schelling, Arms and Influence, 78.\nTHE JOURNAL OF STRATEGIC STUDIES\n\n## Deterrence concepts in cyberspace and U.S. cyber strategy\nWithin the United States, cyber academics and practitioners rely on a variety of deterrence frameworks, some of which rest on implicit assumptions about their causal logics and how they relate to traditional deterrence concepts. This ambiguity makes it difficult to assess the efficacy of cyber strategies and contributes to conceptual confusion. Clarifying these can help to distill differences in the cyber literature about deterrence mechanisms as well as provide clearer policy guidance.⁵³ Table 1 depicts how different cyber deterrence concepts are related to traditional forms of deterrence (denial and punishment) as well as the deterrence mechanisms associated with them (denial, defense, and punishment). The table also articulates the implicit causal logic of each cyber deterrence concept and provides some illustrative examples of how applications of U.S. cyber strategy and policy map onto the different concepts.\nWe argue that, unlike deterrence by punishment – which is hampered by fundamental problems that make the likelihood of success negligible, if not impossible – deterrence by denial is attainable in cyberspace.⁵⁴ Below, we first discuss the infeasibility of deterrence by punishment in cyberspace. Next, we unpack deterrence by denial into its constituent components, defense and denial. We show why, both conceptually and in practice, a deterrence by denial approach based on defense also has significant limitations. Finally, we discuss core elements of a deterrence by denial strategy based on more offensive approach to denial and assess how these relates to the DoD’s defend forward concept. Therefore, our analysis aims to impose rigor on existing deterrence concepts, more narrowly scope which forms of deterrence by denial are feasible, and articulate a path forward for scholars and practitioners.\n⁵¹ Jonathan Shimshoni, *Israel and Conventional Deterrence: Border Warfare from 1953 to 1970* (Ithaca, NY: Cornell University Press, 1988).\n⁵² Snyder, *Deterrence and Defense*, 12–13.\n⁵³ We are grateful to Reviewer 2 for pointing out both the conceptual and policymaking consequences of the ambiguities in existing cyber deterrence concepts.\n⁵⁴ For a discussion of why punishment strategies are unlikely to succeed, see Erica D. Borghard and Shawn W. Lonergan, ‘The Logic of Coercion in Cyberspace’, *Security Studies* 26/3 (2017), 452–481.\n\nTable 1. Deterrence concepts in cyberspace.\n|  Traditional |   | Cyberspace  |   |   |\n| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |\n|  Form of deterrence | Deterrence mechanism\nDenial | Concept\nForward defense | Implied causal logic\nDisrupt, deny, degrade adversary offensive cyber capabilities and strategy, increasing the costs to the opponent of achieving military objectives in cyberspace | U.S. example\nElements of the 2018 DoD ‘defend forward’ concept, particularly some offensive cyber operations  |\n|  Deterrence by denial |  | Cross-domain deterrence | Below the level of armed attack, the employment of diplomatic, law enforcement, and economic tools to constrain an opponent’s ability to carry out cyber campaigns | Department of Justice (DoJ) indictments against specific individuals attributed to cyber attacks  |\n|   |   | Domestic defense/resilience | Increasing the overall ability to defend networks and assets and rapidly recover following disruptive cyber events | Cybersecurity and Infrastructure Security Agency (CISA)’s alerts about cybersecurity information, vulnerabilities, and exploits through the US-CERT  |\n|  Deterrence by denial | Defense | Forward defense | Maneuvering in cyberspace to obtain intelligence about adversary tactics, techniques, and capabilities; attack signatures; and vulnerabilities to provide information and early warning to domestic defenders | DoD ‘hunt forward’ cyber operations  |\n|  Deterrence by punishment | Punishment | Cross-domain deterrence | Above the level of armed attack, the threat to employ the full range of retaliatory military capabilities; below that threshold, the use of economic sanctions | 2018 Nuclear Posture Review suggesting retaliatory response to cyber attacks against nuclear command, control, and communication (NC3).  |\n|   |   | Within-domain deterrence | Above the level of armed attack, the threat to conduct strategic cyber attacks against an opponent’s civilian infrastructure and economy | There is limited public information in this area, but some suggestive reporting about alleged U.S. malware implants in Russia’s power grid.  |\nTHE JOURNAL OF STRATEGIC STUDIES\n\nApplying the logic of deterrence by punishment to cyberspace suffers from challenges of feasibility and credibility. The crux of deterrence by punishment applied to cyberspace entails the threat to respond to a cyber attack by imposing substantial costs against an adversary through cyber means by conducting strategic cyber attacks (within-domain), or through kinetic non-cyber means, which may include conventional or nuclear military force (cross-domain). The premise is that the consequences of the threatened of retaliation are so severe that they would deter the undesired behavior.\nSome parts of U.S. cyber strategy are implicitly grounded in the logic of retaliation for cyber attacks that breach an ambiguously defined threshold of significance. The Obama administration's 2011 International Strategy for Cyberspace articulates a declaratory policy analogous to other domains – that the United States\"reserve[s] the right to use all necessary means,\"including military force, to respond to cyber attacks.[55] The Trump administration's 2018 National Cyber Strategy similarly claims that the United States will leverage\"all instruments of national power,\"specifically including\"military (both kinetic and cyber),\"to respond to cyber attacks.[56] Most directly consistent with a deterrence by punishment logic, a 2017 Defense Science Board report calls for the United States to maintain a cyber-resilience\"thin line\"of critical retaliatory capabilities (including cyber, conventional, and nuclear) as a deterrent to\"the most sophisticated large-scale cyber attacks.\"[57] And the 2018 Nuclear Posture Review, conducted during the Trump administration, implied that the United States could respond with nuclear weapons (most likely limited nuclear options) to cyber attacks that target NC3.[58] Empirically assessing the efficacy of cross-domain deterrence by punishment strategies in cyberspace is challenging because, to date, there has not been a significant cyber attack that has crossed a threshold that has triggered conventional, let alone, nuclear retaliation. It is difficult to evaluate whether the absence of adversary strategic cyber attacks is attributable to effective deterrence by punishment or other, unrelated factors. That said, from a conceptual perspective, the threat of cross-domain punishment mechanisms suffers from significant credibility issues. Specifically, convincing an adversary that a state will respond to a cyber attack with conventional or nuclear capabilities – which would dramatically escalate a crisis – would likely be perceived as implausible, especially given the general absence of physical violence stemming from cyber attacks. Indeed, research by Sarah Kreps and [55] 'International Strategy for Cyberspace: Prosperity, Security, and Openness in a Networked World', *The White House*, May 2011, p. 14.\n[56] 'National Cyber Strategy of the United States of America', *The White House*, September 2018, p. 21.\n[57] 'Department of Defense, Defense Science Board Task Force on Cyber Deterrence', Office of the Undersecretary of Defense for Acquisition, Technology, and Logistics, February 2017, p. 17.\n[58] 2018 Nuclear Posture Review, pp. 31, 57–58.\n# E. D. BORGHARD AND S. W. LONERGAN Jacquelyn Schneider has demonstrated that, in the context of cyber war-games, individuals are\"considerably more restrained when it comes to aggressive retaliatory actions involving the use of force,\"effectively creating a firebreak between cyber and conventional realms.[59] Below a use of force threshold, the United States routinely employs economic measures as a means of imposing costs on adversaries to deter malicious cyber behavior short of war. There is, of course, an a priori question of whether a post-hoc response to an event could even serve deterrence purposes. While punishment after the fact cannot deter an event that has already taken place, it could contribute to deterrence outcomes over the long term by signaling a willingness to respond and clarifying the boundaries of appropriate behavior. Regardless, policymakers have consistency couched economic cost-imposition measures in cyber deterrence terms. A critical juncture for U.S. cyber sanctions policy took place in April 2015 when the Obama administration released an executive order that defined authority for the U.S. to impose sanctions in response to\"malicious cyber-enabled activities.\"[60] Senior administration officials explicitly described the use of sanctions as serving deterrence purposes. Lisa Monaco, then-Assistant to the President for Homeland Security and Counterterrorism, noted that economic sanctions were a critical capability to\"deter malicious cyber activity and to impose costs... on those responsible for significant harmful cyber activity where it really hurts – at their bottom line.\"[61] Since then, economic measures have been an important cyber deterrence tool for U.S. policymakers. According to the think tank Third Way, since 2015, the United States has imposed sanctions against over 300 entities and individuals, mostly affiliated with Russia, North Korea, and Iran, related to malicious cyber behavior.[62] Unlike the threat to use conventional or nuclear force in response to a cyber attack, the threat to impose economic sanctions in response to malicious cyber operations is manifestly credible given the frequency with which the tool is employed. However, this form of deterrence has its own challenges, most notably the poor track record of economic sanctions in general (not only in cyberspace) in altering adversary behavior.[63] In the cyber realm in particular, the U.S. has been hesitant to impose broader, less- 59Sarah Kreps and Jacquelyn Schneider, 'Escalation Firebreaks in the Cyber, Conventional, and Nuclear Domains: Moving Beyond Effects-Based Logics', Journal of Cybersecurity 5/1 (2019), 5. Also see Brandon Valeriano and Ryan C. Maness, 'The Dynamics of Cyber Conflict Between Rival Antagonists', Journal of Peace Research 51/3 (2014), 347–360.\n\n62Allison Peters and Pierce MacConaghy, 'Unpacking US Cyber Sanctions', Third Way, 29 January 2021.\n63See, for example, Robert A. Pape, 'Why Economic Sanctions Still Do Not Work', International Security 22/1, 66–77; Daniel W. Drezner, The Sanctions Paradox (Cambridge University Press, 2010).\nTHE JOURNAL OF STRATEGIC STUDIES Even more problematic is applying punishment concepts to within-domain deterrence; that is, the threat to retaliate with strategic cyber operations in response to a cyber attack. While less explicitly articulated in U.S. policy documents, which tend to couch deterrence declarations in strategic ambiguity about retaliatory options, there is some evidence that part of the U.S. posture includes retaliatory cyber attacks as part of a deterrence approach. For instance, the 2017 Defense Science Board report emphasizes that the United States\"must maintain – and be seen to maintain – an array of scalable offensive cyber capabilities – including high-impact strategic cyber attack options – as an integral part of its cyber deterrence posture.\"64 While more speculative, a number of major media outlets reported in 2018–2019 that the U.S. was considering offensive cyber operations as part of cyber deterrence strategy. For instance, The Washington Post reported in 2018 that then-National Security Advisor, John Bolton, indicated that the United States\"authorized offensive cyber operations\"to\"create structures of deterrence that will demonstrate to adversaries that the costs of their engaging in operations against us is higher than they want to bear.\"65 And The New York Times reported in 2019 that U.S. strategy\"has shifted more toward offense, officials say, with the placement of potentially crippling malware inside [Russia's electric grid] at a depth and with an aggressiveness that had never been tried before. It is intended partly as a warning, and partly to be poised to conduct cyberstrikes if major conflict broke out between Washington and Moscow.\"66 However, there are several challenges associated with applying punishment logics to cyberspace that have been extensively noted in the academic literature. These center on the limitations of offensive cyber operations in inflicting major damage, which is essential for implementing punishment strategies.67 Cyber operations and campaigns, even at scale, simply do not generate costs against a target at a magnitude comparable to that of offensive operations that employ conventional or, certainly, nuclear munitions.68 Most cyber operations are disruptive, causing temporary effects from which targets can rapidly recover. In fact, it is precisely the impermanent and reversible nature of cyber operations that makes them appealing for some 64\"Department of Defense, Defense Science Board Task Force on Cyber Deterrence', Office of the Undersecretary of Defense for Acquisition, Technology, and Logistics, February 2017, p. 15.\n65Ellen Nakashima, 'White House authorizes\"offensive cyber operations\"to deter foreign adversaries', The Washington Post, 20 September 2018.\n66David E. Sanger and Nicole Perlroth, 'U.S. Escalates Online Attacks on Russia's Power Grid,' The New York Times, 15 June 2019.\n67For a review, see Borghard and Lonergan, 'The Logic of Coercion in Cyberspace.'\n68See Erik Gartzke, 'The Myth of Cyberwar', International Security 38/2, 41–73; and Thomas Rid, Cyber War Will Not Take Place (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2013).\n# E. D. BORGHARD AND S. W. LONERGAN actors. The history of cyber conflict largely bears out this argument – most “malicious cyber activity has taken place far below the threshold of armed conflict between states, and has not risen to the level that would trigger such a conflict.” \nThe types of offensive cyber operation that, at a conceptual level, could generate punishment-level costs would be a cyber attack targeting civilian critical infrastructure that is essential to the functioning of a society.[71] However, it stretches credulity that the offensive employment of cyber power could generate the costs necessary for an effective punishment strategy. Offensive cyber operations capable of targeting critical infrastructure (e.g., power grids, pipelines, and transportation nodes, etc.) with sustained, significant effects typically require highly tailored accesses and exploits. These are difficult to scale given the material resources necessary for their development and employment, and it is immensely challenging to continue to generate meaningful costs that can be sustained over time.[72] Recent examples of ransomware attacks against civilian critical infrastructure, such as attacks against pipelines and meat processing plants, have produced only minor, short-term disruptions in service.[73] Therefore, deterrence by punishment that solely relies on offensive cyber operations is likely to be ineffective because cyber warfare is an insufficient means to instill an unbearable level of fear and pain in an adversary.\n# Deterrence by denial Unlike deterrence by punishment, a strategy for cyberspace grounded in the logic of deterrence by denial may be feasible under certain conditions. However, denial has come to mean a range of different things to the point that its meaning has been diluted. At a conceptual level, denial has been used interchangeably with defense, whereas the traditional literature on deterrence by denial draws a distinction between defensive force postures for deterrence (Schelling's\"deterrent defense\"), and defensive forces that are actually capable of defending a state against an aggressor. Relatedly, in Max Smeets and Herbert S.
